First Mu'tazilite Argument: Non-Existents are Distinct
Razi presents the first Mu'tazilite argument that non-existents are distinct, with four proofs and an objection and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument that non-existents are distinct things with real essences, and how they defend it against the objection that distinctions are merely mental.

Core Idea
Non-existents have distinct realities independent of the mind.

Imam Razi presents one of the most foundational Mu'tazilite doctrines: that non-existent things have determinate essences. This lesson walks through their four proofs and their response to the objection that distinctions are only mental.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the literal translation. Get a sense of the overall argument before diving into details.
The passage is dense; focus on the structure: claim, four proofs, objection, answer.
2
Identify the four proofs
The passage gives four distinct proofs (wujuh) that non-existents are distinct. Find each one and notice the reasoning: from knowledge of future events, from power, from desire, and from the division into impossible and possible.
3
Understand the objection and answer
An objector says distinctions are merely mental. Razi's answer distinguishes between the known object and knowledge of it, arguing that the objects themselves are not in the mind.
This is a critical move in the argument.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Razi concludes that the four proofs establish that some non-existents are distinct while non-existent. Consider why this matters for the broader theological debate.
This doctrine underpins Mu'tazilite views on divine knowledge and power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج القائلون بأن المعدوم شيء بأمرين : الحجة الأولى : أن المعدومات متميزة في أنفسها وكل ما يتميز بعضه عن البعض فلا بد وأن تكون لأنفسها حقائق متعينة ولا معنى لقولنا المعدوم شيء إلا ذلك، والذي يدل على أن المعدومات متميزة بعضها عن البعض حال عدمها وجوه الأول : وهو أنا نعلم أن إذا تطلع الشمس من المشرق ولا تطلع من المغرب، وهذان المطلوعان معدومان في الحال، ونحن الآن نعلم امتياز كل واحد منهما عن الآخر، وهذا يدل على وقوع الامتياز في المعدومات. الثاني : هو أنا قادرون على الحركة يمنة ويسرة ولسنا قادرين على الطيران إلى السماء فقد تميز أحد المعدومين عن الثاني من حيث إن أحدهما مقدور لنا والآخر غير مقدور لنا، وهذا الامتياز واقع حال العدم، فثبت وقوع الامتياز في المعدومات. الثالث : هو أنما نجد من أنفسنا أنا نريد أن تحدث لنا أموال وأولاد وخيرات وسعادات وأن لا تحدث لنا أنواع الأمراض والآفات مع كون كل واحد من هذين القسمين معدومًا فلولا تميز أحد المعدومين عن الآخر حال كونه معدوما بماهيته المعينة وحقيقته المخصوصة وإلا لامتنع كون أحدهما مراد الوقوع والآخر مكروه الوقوع. الرابع: هو أن المعدوم قسمان ممتنع ،وجائز ولا شك أن كل واحد من هذين القسمين متميز عن الآخر في نفسه وحقيقته، ولذلك كان القادر المختار لا يمكنه إيجاد الممتنعات ويمكنه إيجاد الممكنات فلولا امتياز الممكن عن الممتنع في نفسه وإلا لما ذلك. صح لا يقال: هذه الأمور وإن كانت معدومة في الخارج إلا أنها موجودة في الذهن؛ فلهذا صح وقوع الامتياز فيها؛ لأنا نقول: إنكم إما أن تقولوا: هذه المعلومات موجودة في الذهن أو تقولوا العلم بها موجود في الذهن، والأول باطل ؛ لأنا إنما نعلم الشمس والقمر، فلو كانت المعلومات موجودة في الذهن لزم فيمن تصور شموسًا كثيرة وأقمارًا كثيرة وبحرا من زئبق وجبلاً من زبرجد أن توجد في ذهنه هذه الأشياء، والقول بفساده معلوم بالضرورة، وأما الثاني، وهو أن الحاضر في الذهن هو العلم بهذه الأشياء فهذا مسلم إلا أن بحثنا عن المعلوم لا عن العلم، فهذه المعلومات لما لم تكن موجودة في الذهن علمنا أنها في أنفسها وحقائقها متميزة، سواء وجدت في الذهن أو لم توجد، وذلك هو المطلوب. فثبت بهذه البراهين الأربعة تميز بعض المعدومات عن البعض حال كونها معدومة.
And those who say that non-existence is something argued by two [arguments]: The first argument: that non-existents are distinct in themselves, and everything whose parts are distinct from each other must have in themselves determinate realities; and there is no meaning to our saying 'non-existence is something' except that. And that which indicates that non-existents are distinct from each other in the state of their non-existence are aspects: First: that we know that the sun rises from the east and does not rise from the west, and these two risings are non-existent at present, and we now know the distinction of each one from the other, and this indicates the occurrence of distinction among non-existents. Second: that we are able to move right and left and we are not able to fly to the sky; so one of the two non-existents is distinguished from the second in that one is within our power and the other is not within our power, and this distinction occurs in the state of non-existence, so the occurrence of distinction among non-existents is established. Third: that we find from ourselves that we desire that wealth, children, goods, and happiness come to us, and that types of diseases and afflictions do not come to us, while each of these two categories is non-existent; were it not for the distinction of one non-existent from the other in the state of its being non-existent by its determinate essence and its specific reality, it would be impossible for one to be desired to occur and the other to be disliked to occur. Fourth: that non-existence is of two types: impossible and possible, and there is no doubt that each of these two types is distinct from the other in itself and its reality, and therefore the powerful chooser cannot bring into existence impossibilities and can bring into existence possibilities; were it not for the distinction of the possible from the impossible in itself, that would not be so. It cannot be said: These things, even if they are non-existent in external existence, are existent in the mind; so for this reason distinction occurs in them. For we say: Either you say that these known things (ma'lumat) are existent in the mind, or you say that knowledge of them is existent in the mind. The first is false, because we only know the sun and moon; if the known things were existent in the mind, it would be necessary for one who imagines many suns and many moons and a sea of mercury and a mountain of chrysolite that these things exist in his mind, and the corruption of this is known necessarily. As for the second, namely that present in the mind is the knowledge of these things, this is granted, but our investigation is about the known (ma'lum), not about knowledge. So these known things, since they are not existent in the mind, we know that they are distinct in themselves and their realities, whether they exist in the mind or not. And that is what is sought. So it is established by these four proofs that some non-existents are distinct from others in the state of their being non-existent.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument (al-hujja al-ula) of those who claim that non-existence is something (al-ma'dum shay'), i.e., the Mu'tazilites. The argument is that non-existents are distinct from each other in themselves, and anything that is distinct must have determinate realities (haqa'iq mu'ayyana). This is what is meant by saying non-existence is something. The author then provides four proofs (wujuh) that non-existents are distinct while non-existent: (1) we know that the sun's rising from east and not rising from west are two distinct non-existent future events; (2) we are able to move right/left but not to fly, so the non-existent movements are distinct in terms of possibility; (3) our desires for future goods and against afflictions show distinction among non-existents; (4) the division of non-existence into impossible and possible shows distinction. Then an objection is raised: these distinctions occur because the things exist in the mind. The answer: either the known objects exist in the mind or knowledge of them exists. The first is false because imagining many suns would require them to exist in the mind, which is impossible. The second is accepted, but the discussion is about the known objects themselves, not knowledge. Hence the objects are distinct in themselves, not merely in the mind. Thus the four proofs establish that some non-existents are distinct while non-existent.

Key Terms
معدوم
متميزة
حقائق
ماهية
مقدور
ممتنع
جائز
ذهن
معلوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Distinction among non-existents is only mental and not real.
Correction: The Mu'tazila argue that non-existents have real distinct essences independent of the mind, as shown by the four proofs.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila, what does the division of non-existence into impossible and possible prove?
A) That non-existents are not distinct.
B) That non-existents have distinct essences.
C) That non-existents are all the same.
D) That impossible things can be created.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite doctrine that non-existents have distinct essences is defended by four proofs and a response to the objection that distinctions are merely mental.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you desire something in the future, do you truly believe that what you desire has a distinct reality even now, in the mind of Allah? Let this realization shift your focus from merely wanting to trusting in the wisdom of what is decreed to exist.
?
You know that you will die one day — a non-existent event that is distinct in its essence. Does this certainty change how you live today? Commit to one concrete action that reflects your belief in that reality.
?
You are able to choose between right and left right now, but not to fly. That distinction is built into the very nature of those non-existent actions. How often do you thank Allah for the possibilities He has given you, rather than lamenting the impossibilities? Pick one impossible thing you wish for and replace that thought with gratitude for a possible action you can take today.

intermediate
4 min
Distinct Non-Existents Have Real Essences
Razi concludes that distinction among non-existents proves they have real essences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that distinction between non-existents implies they have real essences and are not mere nothings.

Core Idea
Distinctness proves that non-existents have real essences.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi concludes his argument that non-existents are distinct from each other, which implies that they must have specific realities and essences. This is a crucial step in the Mu'tazilite position that non-existence is something (al-ma'dum shay'). Razi presents this as part of the opponents' case, to be refuted later, but here we examine the argument itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own flow come through.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if this is established, we say...'
2
Identify the key premises
Find the two key premises: (1) distinction depends on each thing having a specific reality and essence, and (2) distinctness is a ruling/attribute that cannot exist without a constituted subject.
These premises are linked by 'for' (fa-inna).
3
Trace the conclusion
See how from the premises Razi concludes that non-existents are distinct and constituted, and therefore they are essences and realities.
The final sentence states: 'That is what is sought.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : امتياز أحد الأمرين عن الآخر يتوقف على كون كل واحد منهما في نفسه حقيقة معينة وماهية معينة، فإن تميز البعض عن البعض حكم من أحكام تلك الحقيقة وصفة من أوصافها وثبوت الصفة بدوم تقرر الموصوف محال، فثبت أن المعدومات متميزة متقررة، وثبت أن المتميز لا يتحقق إلا عند كون الحقائق والماهيات متقررة، وهذا يوجب القطع بكون المعدومات ذوات وماهيات وحقائق، وذلك هو المطلوب.
And when this is established, we say: The distinction of one thing from another depends on each one being in itself a specific reality and a specific essence. For the distinctness of some from others is a ruling (hukm) of that reality and an attribute of its attributes, and the establishment of an attribute without the constitution of the described is impossible. So it is established that non-existents are distinct and constituted, and it is established that the distinct is not realized except when realities and essences are constituted. And this necessitates the certainty that non-existents are essences (dhawat), essences (mahiyyat), and realities (haqa'iq), and that is what is sought.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage is a conclusion drawn from the previous establishment of distinction among non-existents. It argues that distinction between any two things requires that each has a specific reality (haqiqa mu'ayyana) and essence (mahiyya mu'ayyana). This is because distinctness is a ruling (hukm) and attribute (sifa) of that reality, and an attribute cannot exist without the subject (mawsuf) being constituted (mutakarrira). Therefore, since non-existents are distinct, they must be constituted as realities. This leads to the certainty that non-existents are essences (dhawat), essences (mahiyyat), and realities (haqa'iq). This supports the Mu'tazilite position that non-existence is something (al-ma'dum shay'). Note that in the broader context, Razi is presenting this argument as part of the opponents' case, to be refuted later.

Key Terms
امتياز (distinction)
حقيقة (reality)
ماهية (essence)
حكم (ruling)
صفة (attribute)
متقرر (constituted)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that distinction does not require independent realities.
Correction: The argument claims that distinction does require each non-existent to have a specific reality and essence, otherwise they could not be distinguished.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude from the distinction of non-existents?
A) That non-existents are purely mental.
B) That non-existents have real essences.
C) That non-existents are impossible.
D) That distinction is an illusion.

Key Takeaway
Razi argues that the fact of distinction among non-existents forces us to admit they have real essences, making non-existence something rather than nothing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument shows that even non-existents have real essences known to Allah. When you plan a future action, do you realize that its possibility is not an empty thought but a real essence in God's knowledge? How does that change the seriousness with which you consider your intentions?
?
If distinctness requires a real essence, then every attribute you possess (like your knowledge or will) depends on your created essence. Are you living with the awareness that your very self is constituted by Allah, and that your attributes are not independent?
?
The principle that an attribute cannot exist without a subject is used here. When you attribute goodness to an action, do you recognize that the action itself must have a real essence that grounds that attribute? How does that affect your understanding of moral responsibility?

The Fifth Proof: Quranic Evidence that the Non-Existent is a Thing
Razi cites Quran 18:23 to argue that the non-existent is a thing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Razi uses Quran 18:23 to argue that the non-existent (ma'dum) is considered a 'thing' (shay') in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
The Quran itself calls future actions 'things', proving the non-existent has reality.

Imam al-Razi presents the fifth of his proofs for the reality of the non-existent, this time drawing directly from the Quran. In Surah al-Kahf, Allah instructs the Prophet not to say of a 'thing' that he will do it tomorrow—referring to a future, non-existent action. By using the word 'thing' for something that does not yet exist, the Quran suggests that the non-existent has some ontological status. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how al-Razi cites the verse and then deduces the conclusion.
The verse is from Surah al-Kahf, verse 23.
2
Identify the key terms
Locate 'shay'' and 'ma'dum' in the text. Understand their meanings in this context.
3
Trace the argument
Al-Razi argues: The Quran calls a future action a 'thing'. That action is non-existent at the moment of speech. Therefore, the non-existent is a thing.
This is a direct inference from Quranic usage.
4
Consider the implications
If the non-existent is a thing, it has some reality. This supports the Ash'ari position on the ma'dum.
5
Address a common misunderstanding
Some think the verse implies mental existence. But the proof is about linguistic reference — the word 'thing' applies to future events in ordinary language.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : قوله تعالى: {وَلَا تَقُولَنَّ لِشَيْءٍ إِنِّي فَاعِلٌ ذَلِكَ غَدًا} [الكهف: ٢٣] سمى الأمر الذي سيفعله غدًا في الحال باسم الشيء . وذلك يقتضي أن يكون المعدوم شيئا.
The Fifth Proof: The saying of the Most High: {And do not say of a thing (shay'), 'Indeed I will do that tomorrow'} (Surah al-Kahf, 18:23). He called the matter which He will do tomorrow, in the present moment, by the name 'thing' (shay'). And that entails that the non-existent (ma'dum) is a thing.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof that the non-existent is a thing. It is based on a Quranic verse (18:23): 'And do not say of a thing, "Indeed I will do that tomorrow."' The argument is that in this verse, Allah refers to a future action (which does not yet exist) as a 'thing' (shay'). Since the action is non-existent at the time of speaking, yet it is called a thing, this indicates that the non-existent (ma'dum) can be called a thing and thus has some sort of reality. The proof is straightforward: the Quran itself uses the word 'thing' for a future, non-existent event, so the non-existent must be something.

Key Terms
shay' (thing)
ma'dum (non-existent)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse implies that future actions exist mentally before they occur.
Correction: The proof is about linguistic usage: the word 'thing' is applied to future events in ordinary language, not about mental existence.

Checkpoint
How does the fifth proof use the Quranic verse?
A) It shows that everything that exists is called a thing.
B) It shows that the non-existent is called a thing in the Quran.
C) It proves that actions exist before they happen.
D) It denounces calling future actions 'things'.

Key Takeaway
The Quranic usage of 'thing' for a future action establishes that the non-existent (ma'dum) is considered a thing in Islamic theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I will do this tomorrow,' do you truly regard that future action as a genuine 'thing'—as real as what you are doing now? How does the Quran's usage of 'thing' for a future act challenge you to treat your plans with more seriousness and accountability before Allah?
?
The verse commands you not to say 'I will do that tomorrow' without acknowledging the non-existence of that future act. Yet you make dozens of future promises daily. How many of them do you intend to fulfill? Does this lesson expose a gap between your words and your intentions? What will you change today?
?
If your future sins and good deeds are already called 'things' by Allah, then they are real in His knowledge before you commit them. How does this truth press on your heart now, as you consider what you will do tomorrow? Will you let this awareness govern your choices, or will you ignore it?

Refuting the Scriptural Argument for Nonexistent 'Things'
Razi refutes and counter-proves with another verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam al-Razi argues that the Qur'anic use of 'thing' for nonexistents does not prove they have real essences, and how he uses another verse to support his position.

Core Idea
Linguistic usage does not prove ontological reality.

Imam al-Razi now addresses a scriptural argument brought by opponents who claim that because the Qur'an calls a future event a 'thing,' nonexistents are real entities. Razi carefully distinguishes between mere linguistic labeling and actual ontological status. He then presents his own preferred proof-text to show that nothing but God has eternal existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Observe how Razi structures his refutation.
Notice that Razi quickly concedes the linguistic usage but denies its ontological implications.
2
Identify the two verses
Find the verse the opponents use (al-Kahf 23-24) and the verse Razi uses (al-Baqarah 284). Note what each verse is supposed to prove.
3
Compare the arguments
Understand why Razi says his verse carries meaning (ma'na) while the opponent's only gives wording (lafz). This is a key distinction in kalam methodology between verbal and real evidence.
Razi's conclusion: 'our statement is more appropriate.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحجة الخامسة هي التمسك بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَا نَقُولَنَّ لِشَيْءٍ إِنِّي فَاعِلٌ ذالك غدًا إِلَّا أَن يَشَاءَ اللَّهُ) [الكهف : ٢٣ ٢٤ ] . فهذا يقتضي إطلاق اسم الشيء على المعدوم، ولا يقتضي كون المعدوم ذاتا وماهية وحقيقة، وأما قوله تعالى: ﴿وَاللَّهُ عَلَى كُلِّ شَيْءٍ قَدِرُ) [البَقَرَة : ٢٨٤] فإنه يقتضي وقوع الماهيات بالقادر ، وذلك يفيد نفي كون الذوات والماهيات متحققة الأزل. في والآية التي عول الخصم عليها لا تفيد إلا مجرد اللفظ، والآية التي عولنا عليها تفيد المعنى، فكان قولنا أولى.
As for the fifth proof, it is the adherence to His statement (may He be exalted): 'And do not say of anything, "Indeed I will do that tomorrow," unless God wills' (Surah al-Kahf 23-24). This entails applying the name 'thing' to the nonexistent, but does not entail that the nonexistent is a self, quiddity, and reality. And as for His statement (may He be exalted): 'And God over all things is powerful' (Surah al-Baqarah 284), it entails the occurrence of quiddities from the powerful (God), and that benefits negating that selves and quiddities are realized in pre-eternity. So the verse upon which the opponent relied does not benefit except mere wording, and the verse upon which we relied benefits meaning, so our statement is more appropriate.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage refutes a scriptural argument. The opponents cite the verse 'And do not say of anything, "Indeed I will do that tomorrow," unless God wills' to argue that the word 'thing' is applied to a nonexistent future event, therefore nonexistents are things. Razi replies: this only shows the usage of the word 'thing' as a linguistic expression, not that the nonexistent has a real essence or quiddity. Then he cites his own proof-text: 'And God over all things is powerful.' He argues that this verse indicates that quiddities (essences) occur from the powerful God, which proves that they are not eternal realities in pre-eternity (since only originated things are subject to power). Thus, the opponent's verse only gives a verbal point, while Razi's verse gives a meaningful theological point, making his interpretation preferable.

Key Terms
شيء
معدوم
ذات
ماهية
قادر
أزل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any Qur'anic use of the word 'thing' for a nonexistent proves that the nonexistent has a real essence.
Correction: The Qur'an may use 'thing' for nonexistents as a linguistic expression, but that does not entail ontological reality. A verse must be interpreted in light of its theological meaning.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi consider his own proof-text superior to the opponent's?
A) His verse is more explicit.
B) His verse indicates meaning, not mere wording.
C) His verse is from a later revelation.
D) His verse mentions God's power over nonexistents.

Key Takeaway
The Qur'an's use of 'thing' for nonexistents does not confer ontological reality; true meaning comes from context and theological principles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Read the verse 'And do not say of anything, "Indeed I will do that tomorrow" unless God wills' (Surah al-Kahf 23-24). When you make plans for tomorrow, do you genuinely feel that even the 'thing' you plan is contingent on God's will, or do you assume its existence is certain? What would it mean to truly internalize that every future act is a nonexistent that only God brings into being? Will you start your day tomorrow by explicitly saying 'if God wills' in your heart?
?
The opponent in this lesson assumed that because the Qur'an uses the word 'thing' for a nonexistent, it must be a real entity. Have you ever interpreted a verse literally without considering its deeper theological context? What belief might you be holding onto based on mere wording rather than the intended meaning? Are you willing to ask God to show you if any of your cherished ideas are based on words alone?
?
Razi prioritizes meaning over mere linguistic usage. In your own life, when you read the Qur'an or listen to sermons, do you tend to focus on the surface meaning or do you strive to understand the underlying message? What is one verse you have understood superficially that you will now reexamine with this lesson in mind? Commit to writing down that verse and reflecting on its deeper implications today.

The First Transmitted Objection: The Qur'an as Dhikr
Razi presents the objection that the Qur'an is called dhikr, and all dhikr is temporal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first transmitted objection against the eternality of the Qur'an, namely that it is called dhikr and all dhikr is temporal, and the scriptural evidence used.

Core Idea
The objectors use the Qur'an's own description as dhikr to argue for its temporality.

Razi begins presenting the transmitted objections of those who argue for the temporality of God's speech. This first objection uses a simple syllogism: the Qur'an is called dhikr in multiple verses, and the Qur'an itself states that no dhikr comes except as new (muhdath). Thus, the Qur'an must be temporal. We'll examine the verses cited and the logical structure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
Read the passage and notice the two-part argument: (1) The Qur'an is dhikr, (2) Every dhikr is temporal (muhdath).
This is a syllogism: if the Qur'an is dhikr, and every dhikr is temporal, then the Qur'an is temporal.
2
Examine the scriptural evidence
The objectors cite three verses to prove the Qur'an is dhikr (38:1, 21:50, 43:44) and two verses to prove every dhikr is new (21:2, 26:5). Look up these verses and see how they use the word 'dhikr'.
Notice that the verses for the second premise explicitly say 'muhdath' (newly revealed).
3
Understand the conclusion and its challenge
The conclusion is that the Qur'an is temporal, which challenges the orthodox view that God's speech is eternal. Reflect on why this argument is considered a 'shubha' (specious objection) that requires a response.
Razi will later refute this objection using rational and transmitted arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج القائلون بحدوث كلام الله بالمنقول والمعقول. أما الشبه النقلية : فمن وجوه : الشبهة الأولى : أن القرآن ذِكْر وكل ذكر , محدث، فالقرآن محدث، وإنما قلنا : إن القرآن ذكر ، لقوله تعالى : ﴿ص وَالْقُرْآنِ ذِي الذِّكْرِ﴾ [ص: ١] وقوله تعالى : ﴿وَهَذَا ذِكْرٌ مُبَارَكٌ أَنزَلْنَاهُ﴾ [الأنبياء: ٥٠] وقوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِنَّهُ لَذِكْرٌ لَّكَ وَلِقَوْمِكَ﴾ [الزخرف: ٤٤] وأما أن كل ذكر محدث ففي سورة الأنبياء : ﴿مَا يَأْتِيهِم مِّن ذِكْرٍ مِّن رَّبِّهِم مُحْدَثٍ﴾ [الأنبياء: ٢] وفي سورة الشعراء: ﴿وَمَا يَأْتِيهِم مِّن ذِكْرٍ مِّنَ الرَّحْمَٰنِ مُحْدَثٍ﴾ [الشعراء: ٥].
And those who assert the temporality of God's speech argued by transmitted and rational proofs. As for the transmitted objections, they are from several aspects: The first objection: that the Qur'an is dhikr (reminder), and every dhikr is temporal, so the Qur'an is temporal. We only say that the Qur'an is dhikr because of His statement: 'Sad, by the Qur'an full of reminder' [38:1], and His statement: 'And this is a blessed reminder which We have sent down' [21:50], and His statement: 'And indeed it is a reminder for you and your people' [43:44]. And that every dhikr is temporal is in Surah al-Anbiya: 'No reminder comes to them from their Lord that is newly revealed' [21:2], and in Surah ash-Shu'ara: 'And no reminder comes to them from the Most Merciful that is newly revealed' [26:5].
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage presents the first transmitted (naqli) objection against the eternality of God's speech. The objectors argue that the Qur'an is called dhikr (reminder), and every dhikr is temporal (muhdath). They establish the first premise—that the Qur'an is dhikr—by citing three Quranic verses: Surah Sad (38:1) where the Qur'an is described as 'dhi al-dhikr', Surah al-Anbiya (21:50) where it is called a 'blessed dhikr', and Surah az-Zukhruf (43:44) where it is a 'dhikr' for the Prophet and his people. They then establish the second premise—that every dhikr is temporal—by citing two verses: Surah al-Anbiya (21:2) which says that no 'dhikr' comes from their Lord except it is 'muhdath' (newly revealed), and Surah ash-Shu'ara (26:5) which similarly says no 'dhikr' comes from the Most Merciful except it is 'muhdath'. Therefore, the Qur'an, being a dhikr, must be temporal.

Key Terms
ذكر (dhikr, reminder)
محدث (muhdath, temporal)
شبهة نقلية (transmitted objection)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the objectors are the Asha'ira, but they are the proponents of the creation of the Qur'an (Mu'tazila).
Correction: The objection is raised by those who argue for the temporality of God's speech, typically the Mu'tazila, not the Ash'ari school.

Checkpoint
On what grounds do the objectors claim that the Qur'an is dhikr?
A) They quote verses where the Qur'an is called dhikr.
B) They argue by consensus.
C) They rely on rational proof.
D) They cite hadith.

Key Takeaway
The first transmitted objection attempts to prove the temporality of the Qur'an by arguing that it is called dhikr in the Quran, and all dhikr is described as newly revealed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The objectors selected specific verses to build their argument. Have you ever unintentionally cherry-picked verses to support a belief? What does this lesson teach you about the importance of holistic interpretation?
?
If the Qur'an is truly eternal, why would Allah describe it as 'newly revealed'? Does this tension lead you to deeper trust in divine wisdom or to intellectual discomfort? How will you respond?

Three Logical Inferences from the Verse 'Be!'
Razi presents three logical inferences from 'كن فيكون' to prove God's speech is temporal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three distinct logical arguments derived from the Quranic verse 'Our only word to a thing when We intend it is that We say to it "Be," and it is' (16:40) that are used to argue that God's speech is temporal.

Core Idea
The verse 'Be!' contains three proofs that God's speech is temporal.

Imam al-Razi presents the second transmitted objection against the doctrine of the eternity of the Quran. This objection is based on a single verse, but the opponents derive three separate logical inferences from it. Each inference highlights a different linguistic or logical feature of the verse to demonstrate that God's speech must be created and temporal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Notice that the verse is cited at the beginning, followed by three aspects introduced by 'الأول', 'الثاني', 'الثالث'.
The passage is dense; take your time to parse each aspect.
2
Understand the first aspect
Focus on the conditional structure: the verse is a conditional sentence with 'إذا أردناه' as condition and 'كن' as consequence. The consequence must be after the condition, so speech is after will, hence temporal.
This inference relies on the grammatical necessity of sequence in conditional sentences.
3
Understand the second aspect
Focus on the 'فاء التعقيب' in 'فيكون'. This indicates immediate succession, so 'كن' precedes the created thing without interval, making it temporal.
4
Understand the third aspect
Examine the word 'كن' itself—it is composed of two letters (كاف ونون) that are sequential. This composition implies temporality.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider why the opponents use three different angles from the same verse. Each angle targets a different aspect of the verse's grammar or structure to reinforce the conclusion.
All three aspects converge on the same point: God's speech is created.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثانية : تمسكوا بقوله تعالى: ﴿ إِنَّمَا قَوْلُنَا لِشَيْءٍ إِذَا أَرَدْنَاهُ أَن نَّقُولَ لَهُ كُن فَيَكُونُ ﴾ [النحل : ٤٠] . وجه الاستدلال من ثلاثة أوجه الأول : أن قوله تعالى : ﴿إِنَّمَا قَوْلُنَا لِشَيْءٍ إِذَا أَرَدْنَاهُ أَن نَّقُولَ لَهُ كُن فَيَكُونُ﴾ [النحل : ٤٠] جملة مركبة من شرط وجزاء، والشرط هو قوله : إِذا أَرَدْناهُ والجزاء هو قوله : كُن والجزاء لا بد وأن يكون متأخرًا عن الشرط، فوجب أن يكون قول الله تعالى متأخرًا عن إرادته، والمتأخر عن الغير محدث فوجب أن يكون قول الله محدثاً. الثاني : وهو أن الفاء في قوله : فَيَكُونُ فاء التعقيب، وهذا يقتضي أن يكون المكون حاصلا عقب قوله من غير فصل ولا تراخ، فيلزم أن يكون قوله : كن متقدما على المكون من غير فصل، والمقدم على المحدث بزمان واحد، يجب أن يكون محدثاً، فيلزم أن يكون قوله كن محدثًا. الثالث : أن الآية صريحة في أن قول الله تعالى: كُن كلمة مركبة من الكاف والنون وهما حرفان متعاقبان، فتكون هذه الكلمة محدثة، فيلزم أن يكون قول الله تعالى محدثًا.
The second objection: they hold fast to His statement: 'Our only word to a thing when We intend it is that We say to it "Be," and it is' [16:40]. The aspect of inference is from three aspects: First: that His statement 'Our only word to a thing when We intend it is that We say to it "Be," and it is' [16:40] is a sentence composed of a condition and a consequence. The condition is His statement 'when We intend it' and the consequence is His word 'Be'. The consequence must be posterior to the condition, so the word of God Most High must be posterior to His will, and that which is posterior to another is temporal, so the word of God must be temporal. Second: that the letter fa in His statement 'fayakun' is the fa of immediate succession, and this requires that the thing brought into existence occurs immediately after His word without any interval or delay. Thus His word 'kun' must be prior to the thing brought into existence without any interval, and that which is prior to a temporal thing by an instant must itself be temporal, so His word 'kun' is temporal. Third: that the verse explicitly shows that the word of God Most High 'kun' is a compound word consisting of kaf and noon, which are two sequential letters, so this word is temporal, thus the word of God Most High is temporal.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage presents the second transmitted objection based on the Quranic verse 'Our only word to a thing when We intend it is that We say to it "Be," and it is' (16:40). The objectors derive three distinct logical inferences. The first inference focuses on the conditional structure of the verse: the verse is a conditional sentence with a condition (idha aradnahu) and a consequence (kun). The consequence must come after the condition, so God's speech 'kun' is after His will. Anything that comes after something else is temporal, so God's speech is temporal. The second inference focuses on the particle fa in 'fayakun'. The fa here is the fa of immediate succession (fa' al-ta'qib), indicating that the created thing exists immediately after the word 'kun' without any delay. Therefore, the word 'kun' is prior to the created thing by an instant, and what is prior to a temporal thing is itself temporal. The third inference examines the word 'kun' itself: it is composed of the letters kaf and noon, which follow each other sequentially. Because it is a compound of sequential letters, it is temporal, and thus God's speech is temporal.

Key Terms
قول (word)
إرادة (will)
شرط (condition)
جزاء (consequence)
فاء التعقيب (fa of immediate succession)
مكون (thing brought into existence)
محدث (temporal)
كاف ونون (kaf and noon)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Each aspect is an independent objection.
Correction: All three aspects are derived from the same verse and form one objection.

Checkpoint
What is the first aspect of the second objection?
A) The composition of the word 'kun'
B) The conditional structure making 'kun' posterior to the will
C) The fa of immediate succession
D) The letters kaf and noon

Key Takeaway
The verse 'Be!' provides three distinct logical arguments that God's speech must be temporal, each focusing on a different linguistic or grammatical feature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The verse shows that God's speech 'Be' is contingent upon His will. Do you truly believe that the Quran, as God's speech, is an act of His will—not an eternal attribute? How does this affect your awe of its every verse as a deliberate, timely message for you?
?
Razi argues that the word 'kun' is temporal because it is composed of letters. If you accept that the Quran is created, does your heart still tremble at its words as if they are eternal? What would change if you saw every āyah as a fresh command from God for this moment?
?
The three inferences force you to see the same verse from multiple angles. Are you willing to let the Quran challenge your assumptions about God's nature, or do you read it only to confirm what you already believe? What would it cost you to let the text reshape your theology today?

The Third Objection: Temporal Indicator 'Idh'
Razi presents the third objection based on the temporal word 'idh' in 2:30.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the temporal adverb 'idh' is used as evidence to argue that God's speech is temporal and created.

Core Idea
A time word ties God's speech to a moment—proving it is temporal.

In this objection, the opponent picks a single word from the Quran—'idh' (when)—and argues that its temporal nature proves that God's speech itself is temporal. We will examine the reasoning step by step and see why this is considered a transmitted objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage for the third objection
Read the Arabic text carefully, noting the verse citation and the grammatical term 'ẓarf zamān'.
2
Identify the key premise about 'idh'
Notice that 'idh' is called an adverb of time, which implies a specific time context for the speech event.
3
Follow the logical inference
The argument: anything tied to a specific time must be temporal (ḥādith). Therefore, God's speech in that verse is temporal.
4
Reflect on the implication for the Quran's eternity
If one verse is temporal, then God's speech as a whole could be considered temporal, which contradicts the orthodox belief in the Quran's uncreatedness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثالثة : قوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِذْ قَالَ رَبُّكَ لِلْمَلَائِكَةِ﴾ [البقرة : ٣٠] فكلمة 'إِذْ' ظرف زمان وهذا يدل على أن قول الله تعالى مختص بذلك الوقت، وكل ما كان وجوده مختصا بوقت معين كان محدثًا ، فيلزم أن يكون قول الله تعالى محدثًا. الشبهة الرابعة : أنه تعالى وصف القرآن بقوله : ﴿كِتَابٌ أُحْكِمَتْ آيَاتُهُ ثُمَّ فُصِّلَتْ﴾ [هود : ١] وقال أيضًا: ﴿إِنَّا أَنزَلْنَاهُ قُرْآنًا عَرَبِيًّا﴾ [يوسف: ٢] وهذا يدل على أن القرآن مركب من السور والآيات والحروف والعبارات، ويدل على أن كلام الله تعالى تارة يكون عربيًا وتارة يكون عبرياً، وكل ذلك يدل على أنه محدث مخلوق.
The third objection: His statement: 'And when your Lord said to the angels' [2:30]. The word 'idh' is an adverb of time, and this indicates that the speech of God Most High is specific to that time. Everything whose existence is specific to a particular time is temporal, so the speech of God Most High must be temporal. The fourth objection: that He described the Qur'an with His statement: 'A book whose verses have been perfected and then explained in detail' [11:1], and also said: 'Indeed We have sent it down as an Arabic Qur'an' [12:2]. This indicates that the Qur'an is composed of surahs, verses, letters, and phrases, and indicates that the speech of God Most High is sometimes Arabic and sometimes Hebrew, and all of that indicates that it is temporal and created.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage presents the third and fourth transmitted objections. The third objection uses the verse 'And when your Lord said to the angels' (2:30). The word 'idh' (when) is a time adverb, indicating that the speech occurred at a specific moment. Anything tied to a specific time is temporal, so God's speech in that verse is temporal. The fourth objection uses two verses: 'A book whose verses have been perfected and then explained in detail' (11:1) and 'Indeed We have sent it down as an Arabic Qur'an' (12:2). These descriptions show that the Qur'an is composed of surahs, verses, letters, and phrases, and that it can be in different languages (Arabic, Hebrew). Composition and linguistic variation are characteristics of created, temporal things, so the Qur'an—God's speech—must be created and temporal.

Key Terms
إِذْ (when)
ظرف زمان (adverb of time)
مختص بوقت (specific to a time)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What linguistic feature does the third objection rely on?
A) The word 'kun'
B) The word 'idh' as a time adverb
C) The fa of succession
D) The word 'kitab'

Key Takeaway
The third objection uses the temporal word 'idh' to argue that God's speech is temporal and created.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Does the word 'idh' make God's command seem like a past event, or does it remind you that His speech transcends time? How does your perception affect the way you relate to the Quran today?
?
The objection reduces God's speech to a single moment. Have you ever treated the Quran as only relevant to the past, ignoring its living authority in your present decisions? Can you name one area of your life where you need to let a verse 'speak' to you as if it were said directly to you now?
?
If God's speech is eternal, yet this verse records a specific 'when', how do you reconcile timelessness with a moment in history? Does this challenge you to read the Quran not merely as history but as a divine address that demands a response today? What one change can you make in your recitation to hear it as from the Eternal?

The Fourth Objection: Composition and Language
Razi presents the fourth objection based on the Quran's composition and language variation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quran's composition from parts and its different linguistic forms are used as evidence that it is created.

Core Idea
Anything made of parts and varying in language must be created.

The fourth objection looks at the Quran's own description of itself: it is a 'book perfected and detailed', and it is an 'Arabic Quran'. The opponent argues that these features—composition into surahs, verses, letters, phrases, and the possibility of being in different languages—prove that the Quran is a created entity.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage for the fourth objection
Read the Arabic text, noting the two Quranic verses cited and the list of components that follow.
2
Identify the two types of evidence
The objection first references 'a book whose verses are perfected and detailed' (11:1), then 'an Arabic Quran' (12:2). These show the Quran has structure and language.
3
List the components mentioned
The objection explicitly says the Quran is composed of surahs, verses, letters, and phrases. It also notes that God's speech can be Arabic or Hebrew.
4
Understand the conclusion
Composition and linguistic variation are attributes of created things, so the Quran must be created.

Source Text

The Fifth Objection: The Qur'an is Heard and Therefore Created
Razi presents the fifth textual objection against the eternity of the Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth textual objection against the eternity of the Qur'an, which argues that since the Qur'an is heard and consists of created letters and sounds, it must be created.

Core Idea
What is heard is created; the Qur'an is heard, so it is created.

Imam Razi continues his enumeration of transmitted objections against the eternity of the Qur'an. The fifth objection draws on a straightforward observation: the Qur'an is something that is heard, and what is heard are letters and sounds—both of which are clearly created. Therefore, the Qur'an itself must be created.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the argument.
Pay attention to the verse quoted as evidence.
2
Identify key premises
The objection has three premises: (a) the Qur'an is heard, (b) what is heard is letters and sounds, (c) letters and sounds are created.
3
Understand the conclusion
Notice how the conclusion 'the speech of Allah is created' follows necessarily from these premises.
This is a simple syllogism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الخامسة: أن كلام الله تعالى مسموع ويدل عليه قوله تعالى : ﴿وَإِنْ أَحَدٌ مِنَ الْمُشْرِكِينَ اسْتَجَارَكَ فَأَجِرْهُ حَتَّى يَسْمَعَ كَلَامَ اللَّهِ﴾ [التوبة: ٦] والذي يسمعه ليس إلا هذه الحروف والأصوات، ولا شك أن هذه الحروف والأصوات محدثة، فيلزم القطع بأن كلام الله تعالى محدث.
الشبهة السادسة : أجمعت الأمة على أن القرآن واحد وأجمعوا على أن القرآن معجز والنبي ﷺ والدليل العقلي دل على أن المعجزات يمتنع أن تكون قديمة، بل يجب أن تكون محدثة، وإلا لكانت المعجزات سابقة على الدعوى، وحينئذ لا يكون لها اختصاص بالدعوى، فلا يكون دليلا على صدق الدعوى، وإذا ثبت أن القرآن معجز وثبت أن المعجز محدث ثبت أن القرآن محدث، وإذا ثبت أن القرآن قول واحد ثبت أن كل ما كان قرآنا فهو محدث.
الشبهة السابعة : أن القرآن موصوف بكونه تنزيلا ومنزلاً، وذلك يقتضي كونه محدثًا.
الشبهة الثامنة : صح في الأخبار أنه كان يقول: يارب القرآن العظيم، ويارب طه ويس وكل ما كان مربوبًا فهو محدث مخلوق.
فهذا جملة الكلام في الشبه النقلية.
The fifth objection: The speech of Allah Almighty is heard, and His statement indicates it: 'And if any of the polytheists seeks your protection, then grant him protection so that he may hear the words of Allah' [At-Tawbah 9:6]. And what he hears is nothing but these letters and sounds, and there is no doubt that these letters and sounds are created, so it necessarily follows that the speech of Allah Almighty is created. / The sixth objection: The ummah is agreed that the Qur'an is one, and they are agreed that the Qur'an is a miracle of the Prophet ﷺ, and rational evidence indicates that miracles cannot be eternal, rather they must be created; otherwise, miracles would precede the claim, and then they would have no specific connection to the claim, thus they would not be evidence for the truth of the claim. When it is established that the Qur'an is a miracle and that the miracle is created, it is established that the Qur'an is created. And when it is established that the Qur'an is one speech, it is established that whatever is Qur'an is created. / The seventh objection: The Qur'an is described as being 'sent down' and 'revealed', and that necessitates it being created. / The eighth objection: It is authentically reported in hadiths that he ﷺ used to say: 'O Lord of the Mighty Qur'an! O Lord of Ta Ha and Ya Sin!' And everything that is possessed by a Lord is created and made. / This is the sum of discourse concerning the transmitted objections.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage presents four textual objections (shubah naqliyya) against the doctrine that the Qur'an is eternal (qadim). Each objection draws on specific features of the Qur'an as described in revelation or accepted by the community. The fifth objection: The Qur'an is heard, and hearing pertains to letters and sounds, which are clearly created (muhdath). Therefore, the Qur'an must be created. The sixth objection: The Qur'an is a miracle (mu'jiz) of the Prophet, and rational proof shows that miracles cannot be eternal; otherwise they would precede the claim to prophethood and fail to prove its truth. Since the Qur'an is a miracle, it must be created. Also, the ummah agrees the Qur'an is one, so the whole is created. The seventh objection: The Qur'an is described as 'sent down' (tanzil) and 'revealed' (munzal), implying temporal origination. The eighth objection: The Prophet is reported to have addressed the Qur'an as 'O Lord of the Qur'an', and anything that is possessed by a Lord (marbub) is created and contingent. These four objections are presented without immediate refutation; they are the 'transmitted objections' (shubah naqliyya) against the eternity of the Qur'an.

Key Terms
شبهة نقلية
كلام الله
حروف وأصوات
محدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Qur'an is eternal because it is God's speech.
Correction: This objection shows that if the Qur'an is heard and consists of created letters and sounds, it cannot be eternal.

Checkpoint
What does the fifth objection use as evidence that the Qur'an is heard?
A) A hadith
B) A verse from the Qur'an
C) Rational argument
D) Consensus

Key Takeaway
The fifth objection argues that because the Qur'an is heard and hearing involves created elements, the Qur'an itself must be created.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Qur'an aloud, do you ever pause to think that the very sounds leaving your lips are created? How does this objection force you to examine what you truly believe about the nature of the Qur'an itself? Are you willing to explore the arguments that challenge your comfortable assumption that it is simply eternal?
?
The objector uses the verse 'until he hears the words of Allah' as evidence. Have you ever considered that the verses you cherish might be used to argue against their own eternity? What does this reveal about your readiness to have your beliefs tested? Will you engage with this objection seriously, or dismiss it without thought?
?
This objection reduces divine speech to mere letters and sounds. Does this not disturb you? How can the majestic Qur'an, which transforms hearts, be reduced to created matter? What is your personal response to this reduction of the sacred to the temporal?

The Sixth Objection: The Qur'an is a Miracle and Therefore Created
Razi presents the sixth textual objection against the eternity of the Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth textual objection, which argues that since the Qur'an is a miracle and miracles cannot be eternal, the Qur'an must be created.

Core Idea
Miracles must be created; the Qur'an is a miracle; therefore, the Qur'an is created.

The sixth objection is more elaborate: it combines consensus of the ummah with rational argument. The ummah agrees that the Qur'an is one and that it is a miracle of the Prophet. Rational reasoning shows that miracles cannot be eternal—they must occur after the claim to prophethood to serve as evidence. Hence, the Qur'an is created.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage carefully to understand the two parts: consensus and rational argument.
2
Understand the consensus
The ummah agrees that the Qur'an is one and that it is a miracle of the Prophet.
These are accepted premises.
3
Grasp the rational argument
Rational evidence shows that miracles cannot be eternal; if they were, they would precede the claim and lose evidentiary value.
This is key to the objection.
4
See the conclusion
Therefore, the Qur'an is created. Moreover, because it is one, every part is created.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الخامسة: أن كلام الله تعالى مسموع ويدل عليه قوله تعالى : ﴿وَإِنْ أَحَدٌ مِنَ الْمُشْرِكِينَ اسْتَجَارَكَ فَأَجِرْهُ حَتَّى يَسْمَعَ كَلَامَ اللَّهِ﴾ [التوبة: ٦] والذي يسمعه ليس إلا هذه الحروف والأصوات، ولا شك أن هذه الحروف والأصوات محدثة، فيلزم القطع بأن كلام الله تعالى محدث.

?
If you truly believe the Qur'an is a miracle created to prove the Prophet's truth, does that change how you respond to its commands? Are you living as if the Qur'an is still a living evidence against you?

The Seventh Objection: The Qur'an is Described as Sent Down
Razi presents the seventh textual objection against the eternity of the Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seventh textual objection, which argues that the Qur'an's description as 'sent down' (tanzil) implies it is created.

Core Idea
What is sent down must be created; the Qur'an is sent down; so it is created.

The seventh objection is concise: the Qur'an is repeatedly described in the revelation as 'sent down' (tanzil) or 'revealed' (munzal). The very act of sending down implies temporal origination, for something eternal cannot be 'sent down' at a particular time. Therefore, the Qur'an must be created.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the short Arabic passage and its translation.
2
Reflect on the implication
Consider why being described as 'tanzil' necessitates creation: sending down implies a beginning in time.
This is a linguistic- theological argument.

Source Text

The Eighth Objection: The Prophet's Address to the Qur'an
Razi presents the eighth textual objection against the eternity of the Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the eighth textual objection, which argues that because the Prophet addressed the Qur'an as 'Lord's possession', it must be created.

Core Idea
What is possessed by a Lord is created; the Qur'an is possessed; so it is created.

The eighth objection is based on reported hadiths where the Prophet says: 'O Lord of the Mighty Qur'an!' and similar phrases. These expressions indicate that the Qur'an is something that belongs to Allah (marbub), meaning it is a created entity under His lordship. Anything that is 'marbub' must be created and contingent.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage and translation.
2
Understand the term 'marbub'
This term means 'possessed by a lord' and implies creation.
The hadith is used as evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

Eighteenth Argument: 'Nazar ila' Means Waiting, Not Seeing
Razi argues that 'nazar ila' in Quran means waiting, not literal sight, using linguistic analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses linguistic analysis and rational reasoning to argue that the Quranic phrase 'looking to their Lord' means waiting, not literal sight.

Core Idea
The word 'nazar' in the Quran often means waiting, not seeing.

Imam Razi presents a sophisticated argument that hinges on the linguistic nuance of the word 'nazar' when paired with the preposition 'ila'. By examining the Quranic verse 'to their Lord they are looking', he shows that if it meant literal sight, it would contradict reason, because believers will see many things in the afterlife. Instead, interpreting it as 'waiting' resolves the inconsistency and aligns with both language and theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Razi uses a very structured argument: first stating the claim, then providing evidence.
2
Understand the linguistic principle
Razi begins by asserting that 'nazar' coupled with 'ila' can mean 'waiting'. He then uses a Quranic example where the object 'ila rabbiha' is fronted to indicate restriction (hasr). Identify this principle.
The fronting of the object is key: 'ila rabbiha' comes before the verb to show exclusivity.
3
Analyze the contradiction
If 'nazar' meant seeing, the verse would mean believers see only Allah, but reason tells us they will see Paradise, Hell, etc. This contradiction shows that 'nazar' cannot mean 'seeing' here.
Razi uses rational deduction: what we know about the afterlife contradicts the restriction implied by the verse if nazar = seeing.
4
Appreciate the conclusion
By substituting 'waiting' for 'nazar', the verse makes perfect sense: believers wait only for their Lord's command or mercy. This resolves the apparent contradiction.
This argument is a classic example of combining tafsir and kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة عشرة ومن ههنا نذكر الوجوه الدالة على أن النظر المقرون بحرف إلى قد يجيء بمعنى الانتظار، لا بمعنى الرؤية . : فنقول : قوله تعالى : إِلَى رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ ) [ القِيَامَة : 23] معناه أنها تنظر إلى الرب لا إلى غيره، وذلك لأن تقديم المفعول يفيد الحصر، كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿أَلَا إِلى الله تصيرُ الأُمُورُ ﴾ [الشورى: 53] و عَلَيْهِ تَوَكَّلْتُ وَإِلَيْهِ أنيبُ ﴾ [هود : 88] ، ووَأَنَّ إِلَى رَبِّكَ المنتهى [النجم: 42] وإنَّ إلى رَبِّكَ الرجى العلق : 8] ولا إلَى رَبِّكَ يَوْمَيذٍ الْمَسَاقُ اليقي القِيَامَة : 30] إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : مقتضى الآية أنهم لا ينظرون إلى غير الله تعالى، ولا شك أنهم يرون غير الله تعالى، وذلك لأنهم يرون الجنة والنار ومواقف القيامة، وذلك لأن المؤمنين نظارة في ذلك اليوم ؛ لأنهم هم الآمنون لا خوف عليهم ولا هم يحزنون، ولما دلت الآية على أنهم لا ينظرون إلى غير الله تعالى ودل العقل عليهم ولا يرون غير الله علمنا أن النظر غير الرؤية، أما إذا حملنا هذا اللفظ على الانتظار صح
The eighteenth argument: And from here we mention the points indicating that 'nazar' coupled with the letter 'ila' (to) can come in the sense of waiting (intizār), not in the sense of vision (ru'ya). We say: The saying of Allah Most High: 'Looking to their Lord' [Qiyamah 75:23] means that they look to the Lord and not to other than Him, because the advancement of the object [ila rabbiha] indicates restriction (hasr), as in His saying: 'Indeed, to Allah return all matters' [Shura 42:53] and 'On Him I rely and to Him I turn' [Hud 11:88] and 'And that to your Lord is the finality' [Najm 53:42] and 'Indeed, to your Lord is the return' [Alaq 96:8] and 'To your Lord that Day is the driving' [Qiyamah 75:30]. If this is established we say: The implication of the verse is that they do not 'look' to other than Allah, and there is no doubt that they see (yarūna) other than Allah, because they see Paradise, Hell, and the stations of the Resurrection, because the believers will be onlookers (nuzzār) on that Day, for they are the secure ones, no fear upon them nor do they grieve. Since the verse indicates that they do not 'look' (yanẓurūn) to other than Allah, and reason indicates that they do see (yarūna) other than Allah, we know that 'nazar' is not 'ru'ya'. As for if we interpret this word as waiting (intizār), it becomes valid.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This is the eighteenth argument, which deals with the Quranic verse 'to their Lord they are looking' (إلى ربها ناظرة). Razi argues that 'nazar ila' here means waiting (intizār), not literal sight. He uses linguistic analysis: the phrase 'ila rabbiha' is fronted to indicate restriction (hasr), meaning they look only to the Lord. But believers will undoubtedly see many things in the afterlife—Paradise, Hell, events—so they cannot be restricted to seeing only the Lord if nazar meant seeing. However, if nazar means waiting (for reward or command), then the restriction makes sense: they wait only for their Lord's command or mercy. Therefore, nazar is not ru'ya. This argument combines Quranic exegesis and rational deduction.

First Proof for Seeing Allah: Surah Al-Qiyamah 22-23
Razi presents the first proof for seeing Allah and refutes objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi argues from Surah Al-Qiyamah that believers will see Allah on the Day of Resurrection, and how he refutes alternative interpretations.

Core Idea
The word 'nazar' in the verse must mean vision because the literal meaning is impossible for Allah.

Imam Razi begins the fifth chapter by presenting the first scriptural proof for the beatific vision. He cites the famous verse from Surah Al-Qiyamah and argues that the word 'nazira' (looking) must be interpreted as direct vision. Several objections are raised, challenging the grammar and semantics, but Razi systematically refutes them.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and initial argument
Read the Arabic verse and Razi's initial argument: if 'nazar' means vision, the proof is established; if it means turning the pupil, that is impossible for Allah, so it must be metaphorical for vision.
Notice Razi uses the principle of 'cause for effect' (majaz).
2
Examine the first objection
The opponent argues that 'ila' in the verse is not a preposition but a noun meaning 'blessings', causing 'nazira' to mean 'waiting'. Understand the linguistic support from al-Azhari and poetry.
This objection tries to avoid the vision interpretation entirely.
3
Examine the second objection
Another objection claims 'ila' means 'at' (inda), leading again to 'waiting'. Note the poetic evidence cited.
Both objections aim to reinterpret 'nazira' as waiting.
4
Consider alternative metaphorical interpretations
The opponent proposes two other possibilities: implicit addition of 'reward' (looking at reward) or benefit (waiting). Understand why these are considered alternative majaz.
The opponent tries to show that Razi's preferred majaz is not unique.
5
Study Razi's refutations
Razi refutes each: waiting entails grief (unbefitting the joyful day), implicit addition is worse than figurative speech, and looking at reward requires multiple assumptions.
This solidifies the argument that direct vision is the only coherent interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الخامس: في إقامة الدلالة على أن المؤمنين يرون الله تعالى يوم القيامة. ويدل عليه وجوه: الحجة الأولى: قوله تعالى: ﴿وُجُوهٌ يَوْمَئِذٍ نَاضِرَةٌ إِلَى رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ﴾ [القيامة: 22-23]. فنقول: النظر إن كان هو الرؤية، فالمطلوب حاصل. وإن كان عبارة عن تقليب الحدقة إلى جهة المرئي، فنقول: هذا في حق الله تعالى محال، فوجب حمل لفظة النظر على الرؤية إطلاقا للفظ السبب على المسبب، فإن النظر سبب للرؤية، ولأن المقصود من تقليب الحدقة إنما هو الرؤية.

فإن قيل: لا نسلم أنه حصل في هذه الآية لفظ النظر مقرونا بحرف إلى. وقوله: إلى ربها ناظرة [القيامة: 23] فلا نسلم أن (إلى) ههنا من حروف الجر، بل هو عندنا اسم. وبيانه من وجهين: الأول: أنه واحد الآلاء، على ما ذكره (الأزهري) في كتاب (التهذيب). قال الشاعر وهو (الأعشى): أبيض لا يرهب الهزال ولا يقطع رحما، ولا يخون إلى. أي لا يخون نعمه. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول: إنا توافقنا على أن لفظ (ناظرة) إذا كان عاريا عن حرف (إلى) أفاد معنى الانتظار، كقوله تعالى: ﴿فَنَاظِرَةٌ بِمَ يَرْجِعُ الْمُرْسَلُونَ﴾ [النمل: 35]. إذا عرفت هذا فتقول لم لا يجوز أن يكون تقدير الآية: وُجُوهٌ يَوْمَئِذٍ نَاضِرَةٌ إِلَى رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ [القيامة: 22-23] أي نعمة ربها منتظرة؟

الثاني: إن لفظ (إلي) جاء بمعنى (عند)، قال الشاعر وهو (أوس): فهل لكم فيما إلي فإنني طبيب بما أعيي النطاسي حذيما؟ أي فهل لكم فيما عندي؟ إذا ثبت هذا فلم لا يجوز أن يكون تقدير الآية: وُجُوهٌ يَوْمَئِذٍ نَاضِرَةٌ [القيامة: 22] عند ربها ثم قال بعد ذلك (ناظرة) أي منتظرة وهو خبر عن الوجوه والتقدير: وجوه يومئذ ناضرة عند ربها منتظرة نعمة ربها؟

سلمنا: أنه حصل في هذه الآية لفظ النظر مقرونا بحرف إلي، لكن لا نسلم أنه للرؤية. قوله: (لفظ النظر لتقليب الحدقة، ولا يمكن حمله ههنا على هذا المعنى، فوجب حمله على لازمه، وهو الرؤية). قلنا: حصل ههنا وجهان آخران من المجاز: الأول: إضمار المضاف. والتقدير إلى ثواب ربها ناظرة. والثاني: أن تقليب الحدقة إلى جهة. كما يلزمه الرؤية، فكذلك يلزمه نوع النفع، وهو الانتظار. فلم كان حمل اللفظ على المجاز الذي ذكرتم أولى من حمله على أحد هذين المجازين؟

والجواب: أما حمل لفظ إلى واحد الآلاء، أو على معنى عند. فإنه يقتضي حمل قوله (ناظرة) على الانتظار. وذلك غير جائز لأن الانتظار يلزمه الغم، كما قيل: الانتظار الموت الأحمر، والبشارة بما يوجب الغم غير لائقة بالحكمة. قوله: المراد من الآية إلى ثواب ربها ناظرة. قلنا: هذا مدفوع من وجهين: الأول: إن ما ذهبتم إليه يوجب الإضمار في الآية. وما ذهبنا إليه يوجب المجاز. والمجاز خير من الإضمار - على ما بيناه في أصول الفقه. والثاني: أن النظر إلى الثواب لا يفيد الفرح. فلا بد من إضمار زيادة أخرى. وهي كون ذلك الثواب واصلا إليه. وتكثير الإضمارات خلاف الأصل. قوله: نحمله على الانتظار. قلنا: إنه غير جائز.
The fifth chapter: On establishing the proof that the believers see Allah Almighty on the Day of Resurrection. And there are aspects that indicate it: The first proof: His saying, the Exalted: 'Faces on that Day will be radiant, looking at their Lord' [Al-Qiyamah: 22-23]. We say: If 'looking' (al-nazar) is vision (al-ru'ya), then the demanded is attained. And if it is an expression of turning the pupil towards the direction of the seen, we say: this is impossible regarding Allah Almighty, so it is necessary to carry the word 'looking' upon vision, applying the word of the cause to the effect, for looking is a cause for vision, and because the purpose of turning the pupil is only vision.

If it is said: We do not concede that in this verse the word 'looking' occurred connected with the particle 'ila'. And His saying: 'looking at their Lord' [Al-Qiyamah: 23]; we do not concede that 'ila' here is one of the prepositions, but rather it is with us a noun. Its explanation is from two aspects: The first: that it is one of the 'ala' (blessings), according to what al-Azhari mentioned in the book al-Tahdhib. The poet al-A'sha said: 'White, he does not fear emaciation, nor does he cut a womb, nor does he betray 'ila' (i.e., he does not betray His blessings).' If this is established, we say: We have agreed that the word 'nazira' when it is devoid of the particle 'ila' conveys the meaning of waiting, as His saying, the Exalted: 'Then they are waiting to see with what the messengers return' [Al-Naml: 35]. If you understand this, then you say: Why is it not permissible that the estimation of the verse be: 'Faces on that Day will be radiant, looking at their Lord' [Al-Qiyamah: 22-23] i.e., awaiting the blessing of their Lord?

The second: That the word 'ila' occurs meaning 'inda' (at/near). The poet Aws said: 'Do you have any of what is with me, for I am a physician with what baffles the skilful doctor?' i.e., do you have what is with me? If this is established, then why is it not permissible that the estimation of the verse be: 'Faces on that Day will be radiant' [Al-Qiyamah: 22] with their Lord, then after that He said 'looking' i.e., waiting, and it is a predicate of the faces, and the estimation: Faces on that Day radiant with their Lord, awaiting the blessing of their Lord?

We concede: that in this verse the word 'looking' occurred connected with the particle 'ila', but we do not concede that it is for vision. His saying: 'The word 'looking' is for turning the pupil, and it cannot be carried here upon this meaning, so it must be carried upon its necessary concomitant, which is vision.' We say: There occur here two other aspects of figurative speech (majaz): The first: implicit addition of the annexed noun. And the estimation: looking at the reward of their Lord. The second: That turning the pupil towards the direction, just as it entails vision, so too it entails a kind of benefit, which is waiting. So why is carrying the word upon the figurative speech which you mentioned more appropriate than carrying it upon one of these two figurative speeches?

And the answer: As for carrying the word 'ila' as one of the 'ala', or upon the meaning of 'inda', it requires carrying His saying 'looking' upon waiting. And that is not permissible because waiting entails grief, as it is said: 'Waiting is the red death', and giving news that entails grief is not befitting wisdom. His saying: 'The intended meaning of the verse is looking at the reward of their Lord.' We say: This is refuted from two aspects: The first: What you have gone to requires implicit addition (idmar) in the verse, and what we have gone to requires figurative speech (majaz). And figurative speech is better than implicit addition, as we have explained in Usul al-Fiqh. The second: That looking at the reward does not bring joy. So there must be an additional implicit addition, which is that that reward reaches him. And multiplying implicit additions is contrary to the principle. His saying: 'We carry it upon waiting.' We say: It is not permissible.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage presents the first scriptural proof for the beatific vision (ru'yat Allah) from Surah Al-Qiyamah. The author, Imam Razi, begins by citing the verse: 'Faces on that Day will be radiant, looking at their Lord.' He then offers an initial argument: the word 'nazar' (looking) in the verse either means vision itself or the physical act of turning the eye. The latter is impossible regarding Allah, so it must be interpreted as vision, the intended result. This is a common kalam argument: the literal meaning is impossible, so the metaphorical meaning (the necessary concomitant) is adopted.

An opponent then raises several objections. First, he claims that the word 'ila' in the verse is not a preposition but a noun meaning 'blessings' (a'laa), citing al-Azhari and a line of poetry. If so, the construction would be 'a looking of blessings' meaning 'awaiting blessings.' Second, he suggests 'ila' could mean 'at/near' ('inda), also citing poetry. In that case, the faces are 'radiant near their Lord, awaiting.' In both cases, the word 'nazira' would then mean 'waiting' (intidhar) rather than seeing.

Razi then concedes that 'nazar' appears with 'ila' but still denies that it means vision. The opponent argues that since 'nazar' literally means turning the pupil, and that is impossible for Allah, it must be metaphorically applied to its necessary concomitant, vision. But the objector counters with two alternative metaphorical interpretations: either implicit addition of 'reward' (i.e., 'looking at the reward of their Lord'), or taking the turning of the pupil as implying benefit (waiting) rather than vision. Why should the opponent's preferred metaphorical reading be chosen over these?

Razi answers by showing the flaws in these alternatives. Interpreting 'ila' as blessings or as 'at' forces 'nazira' to mean 'waiting.' But waiting entails anxiety and grief, which is unbefitting the joy of the Day of Resurrection. As the proverb says, 'Waiting is the red death.' So such an interpretation is inconsistent with divine wisdom. As for the interpretation 'looking at the reward,' Razi points out it requires implicit addition (idmar) of 'reward,' while his own interpretation requires only figurative speech (majaz). According to principles of usul al-fiqh, majaz is preferable to idmar. Moreover, looking at the reward does not in itself guarantee joy; one must also implicitly add that the reward is actually received. That multiplies implicit additions, which is contrary to the principle of avoiding unnecessary assumptions. Therefore, the only sound interpretation is that 'nazar' here means direct vision of Allah.

Key Terms
رؤية الله
النظر
الحقيقة والمجاز
الإضمار
الانتظار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'nazar' in the Quran always means physical seeing or waiting, and that the verse in Surah Al-Qiyamah does not prove direct vision of Allah.
Correction: Razi demonstrates that the literal meaning (turning the pupil) is impossible for Allah, and alternative interpretations (waiting) lead to contradiction with divine wisdom or require unnecessary assumptions, so the only coherent interpretation is direct vision.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi consider interpreting 'nazira' as 'waiting' (intidhar) to be invalid?
A) Because the grammar of the verse does not allow it
B) Because waiting entails grief, which is unbefitting the joy of that day
C) Because the word 'nazira' never means waiting in the Quran
D) Because the Prophet explicitly said it means vision

Key Takeaway
The only sound interpretation of 'nazira' in Surah Al-Qiyamah is direct vision of Allah, as alternatives lead to contradiction or unnecessary assumptions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi argues that 'waiting' implies grief, and that giving news that brings grief is unbefitting divine wisdom. When you anticipate meeting your Lord, do you feel more like a hopeful 'waiter' or a joyful 'seer'? What change in your perspective would this verse demand if you truly believed you will see Him?
?
The objector proposes alternative interpretations to avoid the plain meaning of seeing Allah. Are there areas in your faith where you subtly resist a direct encounter with Allah's attributes, preferring to 'add' layers of interpretation? What might that reveal about your heart's comfort level with the reality of His presence?
?
If 'looking at the reward' requires multiple assumptions, but 'looking at Allah' requires only one, why do we sometimes prefer complicated explanations over simple truths? In your own spiritual life, what 'reward' have you been looking at instead of the Giver Himself?
